class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">106.6 @ 5C</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">107.8, 1000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">no decay</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-tbl1-fn1"><div class="footnote" id="tbl1-fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Na(Cr<sub><i>x</i></sub>M<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>)O<sub>2</sub> compounds with <i>x</i> ≪ 0.5 were excluded in doped NaCrO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="tbl1-fn2"><sup><sup>b</sup></sup><p class="last">1C = 120 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></div></div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All chemicals (purity &gt;99.5%) were purchased from Merck. NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and <i>x</i>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated Na(Cr<sub>1–2<i>x</i></sub>Al<sub>2<i>x</i></sub>)O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.01, 0.025, and 0.5) were prepared using a sol–gel method with citric acid as a chelating agent. To synthesize these materials, NaNO<sub>3</sub>, Cr(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, and Al(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O (molar ratio = 1:1:<i>x</i>) were dissolved in 50 mL of water. Citric acid was then added to the solution, which was stirred at 70 °C for several hours and then dried at 150 °C overnight in a vacuum oven. The resulting black-green precursor was decarbonized at 500 °C in air for 6 h and sintered at 900 °C under an Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (5%) atmosphere to obtain NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and <i>x</i>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated Na(Cr<sub>1–2<i>x</i></sub>Al<sub>2<i>x</i></sub>)O<sub>2</sub> powders. For comparison, Na(Cr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> and 0.025Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCrO<sub>2</sub> were also synthesized through the same procedure. To prevent phase change and surface reaction due to ambient moisture, all synthesized products were stored in an argon-filled glovebox.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Characterizations and Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and <i>x</i>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated Na(Cr<sub>1–2<i>x</i></sub>Al<sub>2<i>x</i></sub>)O<sub>2</sub> were obtained using a COXEM EM-30<sup>N</sup> analyzer. Ex-situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the synthesized powders were recorded at a scan rate of 5° min<sup>–1</sup> using a Rigaku ULTIMA 4 diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) studies were conducted using a Thermo Fisher (K-alpha) electron spectrometer with a Kα X-ray source (excitation energy 1486.6 eV). The scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) image, corresponding energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) maps, high-resolution TEM images, and electron diffraction patterns of 0.01Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were obtained using a Cs-corrected field-emission TEM (JEOL, JEM-ARM200F, 200 kV). In operando XRD patterns for NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and 0.01Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were collected on the 9A-U-SAXS XRD beamline at PLS-II at POSTECH in Pohang, South Korea. XRD patterns were recorded as a set of circles on a Mar 345 image plate detector in transmission mode for 7 s. The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) curves were recorded using a Hitachi STA7200 at a scan rate of 5° min<sup>–1</sup> under an argon atmosphere. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) data were collected using a Nicolet iS10 spectrometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific).</div><div class="NLM_p last">Cathode composites were prepared by mixing the active material (80 wt %) with carbon black (10 wt %, Super P) and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (10 wt %, HSV900, MTI) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone. The slurry was coated on aluminum foil and vacuum-dried for 6 h. The active mass loading of cathode materials was 2–3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Coin cells (CR2032, Wellcos) were assembled with sodium metal as the anode. The electrolyte was a mixture of ethylene carbonate and dimethyl carbonate (EC:DMC = 1:1) containing 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>. All of the processes for electrode preparation and cell assembly were performed in an argon-filled glovebox (O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 1 ppm). Galvanostatic C/D cycle tests were performed using an automatic WBCS 3000 battery cycler (WonATech).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Simultaneous Formation of a Core–Shell Structure in a Single Pot</h3><div class="NLM_p">Motivated by the partial success of either isovalent/aliovalent doping or surface coating (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>), we investigated the synergistic contribution of these two effects to the electrochemical properties of NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. To do this, we performed a one-pot synthesis, rather than a two-step synthesis, and examined the spontaneous formation of an Al-doped NaCrO<sub>2</sub> core and a Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell. Simply, Al precursors were added to the solution that contained chromium and sodium precursors at a stoichiometric ratio. The amount of Al was varied to be 2, 5, and 10 mol % of Cr, resulting in the synthesis of 0.01Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 0.025Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and 0.05Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.90</sub>Al<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Hereafter, these compounds will be denoted as 1C-NCAO, 2.5C-NCAO, and 5C-NCAO, respectively. The bare NaCrO<sub>2</sub> will be denoted as NCO. All of the synthesized compounds through the sol–gel method possessed a plate-like morphology, reflecting the characteristics of layered compounds (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The XRD patterns of the as-prepared NCO and <i>x</i>C-NCAO (<i>x</i> = 1, 2.5, and 5) are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>A. All of the patterns of <i>x</i>C-NCAO showed no significant deviations from the peak positions and intensities of NCO, indicating the retention of the O3-type structure of NCO. Upon adding Al, however, small new peaks began to appear and became more prominent as the Al content increased. These new peaks corresponded to the most intense diffraction peaks of α-Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> ((012) and (116)), which implied the phase separation of a mixture into Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and NCO-based phases. No evidence for the presence of Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> was observed.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (A) Comparison of XRD patterns of <i>x</i>C-NCAO. (B) Full-pattern Rietveld refinement of 1C-NCAO. The red dots, black line, and blue line represent observed, calculated, and difference profiles, respectively. The vertical green and pink tick marks denote the position of Bragg reflections of NCAO and α-Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, respectively. (C) Evolution of lattice dimensions with <i>x</i> in <i>x</i>C-NCAO and mol % of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> obtained from the refinements.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To confirm the incorporation of Al into NCO, Rietveld refinements were conducted on the XRD patterns. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>B shows the fitting profiles for 1C-NCAO as a representative example. The profiles for the rest (NCO, 2.5C-NCAO, and 5C-NCAO) are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, and the refined parameters for all compounds are tabulated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–S4</a>. In order to achieve the best fitting quality, α-Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> was also included during the refinement process. As a result, the figures of merit were reduced to an acceptable level, and the goodness of fit (χ<sup>2</sup>) was less than 3.0 for all compounds. The lattice parameters obtained via Rietveld refinements revealed a gradual contraction of unit cell dimensions (a continuous decrease of the <i>a</i>- and <i>c</i>-axis lengths and unit cell volumes) with the addition of Al (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>C). This behavior suggested the substitution of small-sized Al<sup>3+</sup> (0.535 Å) for large-sized Cr<sup>3+</sup> (0.615 Å), following Vegard’s law. More interestingly, the Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> fractions closely matched half of the mol % of Al added, which validated the formation of two phases (<i>x</i>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Na(Cr<sub>1–2<i>x</i></sub>Al<sub>2<i>x</i></sub>)O<sub>2</sub>) and excluded the possibility of the presence of other phases (e.g., Cr<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Al<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell or Na-deficient NCAO).</div><div class="NLM_p">We next confirmed that the as-synthesized compound is not a mixture of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and NCAO, but rather it is composed of a Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell and an NCAO core. To demonstrate this, we analyzed the changes in surface composition with the addition of Al using XPS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>A). It was clear that the Na<sub>1s</sub> peak intensities suddenly decreased upon adding Al. Compared to the peak intensity of Na<sub>1s</sub> in NCO, the peak intensities in 1C-NCAO, 2.5C-NCAO, and 5C-NCAO were reduced to 0.35, 0.23, and 0.20, respectively. If the compound were simply a mixture of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and NCAO, the peak intensities of Na 1s would have steadily decreased with increasing Al content (e.g., 98%, 95%, and 90%). The sudden decrease of Na<sub>1s</sub> indicates the presence of NCAO as a core. In contrast, the peak intensities of Cr<sub>2p</sub> showed the opposite behavior. Compared to the intensity in NCO, the intensities of Cr<sub>2p</sub> were increased to 1.35, 1.82, and 3.03 in 1C-NCAO, 2.5C-NCAO, and 5C-NCAO, respectively. Because the elemental concentrations of Cr are nearly invariant, the significant increase of Cr<sub>2p</sub> intensities clearly indicates the formation of a Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell on the surface of NCAO. Reflecting the core–shell structure, Al<sub>2p</sub> peaks also showed a nonlinear behavior with Al addition. For example, the peak intensity of Al<sub>2p</sub> in 2.5C-NCAO was only 1.12 times as strong as that in 1C-NCAO, despite a 2.5-fold increase in Al content. We also examined the changes in the concentrations of the constituent elements with depth in 1C-NCAO. We monitored the variations in Na<sub>1s</sub> and Al<sub>2p</sub> intensities relative to Cr<sub>2p</sub> intensity with Ar-plasma etching time in survey XPS spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). As expected, we observed a gradual increase in the Na<sub>1s</sub> and Al<sub>2p</sub> peak intensities with etching time, indicating the presence of the Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> as a surface layer.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (A) XPS spectra of Na, Cr, and Al for NCO and <i>x</i>C-NCAO. (B) STEM image and corresponding EDX maps of 1C-NCAO, showing the homogeneous distribution of constituents and quantitative agreement of relative atomic % with nominal compositions. (C) High-resolution TEM images of 1C-NCAO, showing the presence of a surface shell with a thickness of 5 nm. (D) ED patterns on the periphery (square in (C)), demonstrating that the surface layer consists of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The relative atomic % of the constituting elements, as well as the formation of a Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell and an NCAO core, was also confirmed by STEM. For this analysis, a thin, plate-shaped particle of 1C-NCAO on a carbon holey grid was chosen for the examination of the lattice fringes at the edge. The EDX maps showed the atomic % of 24.5 ± 0.3% for Na, 24.4 ± 0.2% for Cr, 0.6 ± 0.2% for Al, and 50.5 ± 0.9% for O, which nearly corresponded to the relative atomic ratio in 1C-NCAO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>B). While Na, Cr, and O were homogeneously distributed, the distribution of Al appeared to be slightly localized due to its relatively small amounts. In addition, a high-resolution TEM image was taken of the particle edge to confirm the existence of the Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>C). A clear darkness contrast was observed, with a brighter peripheral layer approximately 5 nm thick being evident. To further confirm the presence of the Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating layer, an electron diffraction (ED) pattern was obtained from the edge of the particle. The indexed ED pattern of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> along the [001] zone axis is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>D. The diffraction spot patterns were well indexed and matched with (<i>hk</i>0) reflections of α-Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (space group = <i>R</i>3̅<i>c</i>).</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Excellence of 1C-NCAO in Cyclic Stability</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical stability of <i>x</i>C-NCAO was tested during galvanostatic C/D at a rate of 0.2C (24 mA g<sup>–1</sup> based on 1C = 120 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) in a solution of 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC using Na metal as the counter electrode. To highlight the excellence of <i>x</i>C-NCAO, pristine NCO, NCO coated with 0.025 Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (no Al-doped), and Al-doped NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (noncoated) were also subjected to the same C/D cycles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A shows the change in C/D profiles of NCO during 100 cycles, which indicates that although the high-voltage cutoff was limited to 3.6 V (i.e., Na<sup>+</sup> extraction up to Na<sub>∼0.5</sub>CrO<sub>2</sub>), the cyclability of NCO was far from satisfactory, likely due to continuous Cr dissolution during repeated C/D cycles and concomitant formation of inactive P′3 phases (see below). The first discharge capacity of 112.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was continuously decreased to 76.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 C/D, with a fading rate of −0.36 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> cycle<sup>–1</sup>, which was similar to the value previously reported for non-nanometric NCO. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> When coated with Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, the improvement in the retention of NCO was noticeable as previously reported (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>B). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The first discharge capacity of 105.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> decreased to 93.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 C/D, resulting in a fading rate of −0.12 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> cycle<sup>–1</sup>. The cyclic stability of Al-doped NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was also compared (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>C). A similar positive effect on cyclability was observed after Al incorporation with a slight decrease in the first discharge capacity (103.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) but a noteworthy improvement in the fading rate (−0.10 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> cycle<sup>–1</sup>). Although there was a significant improvement in cyclic stability, these fading rates (−0.12 and −0.10 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> cycle<sup>–1</sup> in NCO coated with 0.025 Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, respectively) suggest that further improvement is still needed. It should also be mentioned that both increasing and decreasing the Al content resulted in a decrease in the reversible capacity value and cyclic stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). NaCr<sub>0.90</sub>Al<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> showed better cyclability (−0.07 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> cycle<sup>–1</sup>), but the initial capacity was limited to only 96.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> delivered 108.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> during the first cycle, but cyclic stability became worse (−0.15 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> cycle<sup>–1</sup>). While the gradual decrease in capacity with increasing Al contents is understandable, the increase in cyclability was interesting because it appeared to be related to the suppression of the disproportionation reaction (vide infra).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Representative voltage profiles for (A) NCO, (B) 0.025Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NCO, (C) NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, (D) 2.5C-NCAO, (E) 1C-NCAO, and (F) 5C-NCAO during 100 C/D cycles at 0.2C. (G) Comparison of long-term cyclability of 1C-NCAO and NCO at 5C. Comparison of rate capability of (H) 1C-NCAO and (I) NCO. Cells were subjected to 100 C/D cycles at 5C prior to rate tests in (H) and (I). Solid and open marks indicate charge and discharge capacities, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">We then investigated C/D behaviors of <i>x</i>C-NCAO to confirm the potential synergistic improvement from simultaneous Al doping and Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating. Indeed, 2.5C-NCAO (0.025Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) showed an enormous contribution to cyclic stability. There was very little fading in discharge capacities during the initial two C/D cycles (98.6 → 96.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), followed by virtually no fading with a constant capacity of ca. 96 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> during subsequent cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>D). The Coulombic efficiency was also nearly 100% except the initial few cycles. To elucidate the effect of Al doping and Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating on the kinetic process, cyclic voltammograms of 0.025Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NCO, NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and 2.5C-NCAO were compared with that of pristine NCO for the second cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The results show that the peak potential and peak separation (Δ<i>E</i><sub>p</sub>) were almost identical, with only a slightly wider Δ<i>E</i><sub>p</sub> of up to 30 mV observed for the coated samples. This suggests that the Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics remain largely unchanged by the coating and/or doping in the concentration range that was studied.</div><div class="NLM_p">While the simultaneous coating and doping resulted in excellent cyclability in 2.5C-NCAO, it was compromised by the reduced capacity due to the inclusion of electroinactive Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shells and Al<sup>3+</sup> dopants. Therefore, we synthesized 1C-NCAO (0.01Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) to increase the discharge capacity while maintaining cyclic stability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>E). As expected, 1C-NCAO delivered an enhanced discharge capacity of 107.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> during the first cycle, which was slightly lower than the value for pristine NCO. The capacity was slightly reduced to 106.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the second cycle, but no further fading was observed from the third cycle. Interestingly, the overvoltage was also continuously reduced with repeated cycling, resulting in a discharge voltage that increased by ca. 0.1 V. Note that 0.5C-NCAO (0.005Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.99</sub>Al<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) with less amount of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> resulted in inferior cyclic stability to 1C-NCAO, which could be due to insufficient coating (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). 5C-NCAO (0.05Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NaCr<sub>0.90</sub>Al<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) with thicker Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> layers and a greater amount of Al<sup>3+</sup> dopants than 2.5C-NCAO also showed excellent cyclic stability, but the problem associated with the reduction in reversible capacities was more pronounced (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>F). The comparison of C/D profiles of <i>x</i>C-NCAO suggests that 1C-NCAO, which is composed of the thinnest Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> layer and NCAO doped with the appropriate amount of Al<sup>3+</sup>, is optimal for high cyclability with no significant loss of capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">The long-term stability of 1C-NCAO was also impressive, as monitored at a higher C/D rate. During 1000 C/D cycles at 5C, 1C-NCAO showed no worsening in reversible capacities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>G). Instead, the initial decrease in capacity (106.6 → 102.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) was followed by a gradual increase during subsequent C/D cycles. As a result, 1C-NCAO maintained capacities at ca. 107–108 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> during most C/D cycles. In sharp contrast, NCO, which had a similar discharge capacity of 108.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> during the first C/D cycle, experienced a significant decrease in capacity with cycling, resulting in 64 and 56 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 and 500 C/D cycles, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">1C-NCAO also showed the maintenance of rate capability, which is one of the advantageous characteristics of NCO. Despite the presence of a coating layer, the reversible capacity of 1C-NCAO was not significantly decreased at high C-rates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>H). The capacity of 99.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was delivered at 10C, which amounted to 91% of that at 0.1C. The reversal of C-rate to 0.1C after cycling at 5C also showed an immediate recovery of capacity, reflecting excellent cyclic stability. For comparison, NCO was also subjected to the same sequence of stepwise rate changes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>I). Note that the test was performed with electrodes (1C-NCAO and NCO) that had been cycled 100 times at 5C in order to eliminate the complexity caused by capacity reduction with cycling, particularly in NCO. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>I shows that while the rate capability is still noteworthy, the overall capacity is inferior to that of 1C-NCAO. The capacities at 1C and 10C were only 66.7 and 54.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, indicating that certain types of side reactions occurred on the NCO surface during precycling (100 C/D cycles).</div><div class="NLM_p">To understand the relationship between capacity retention and structural reversibility, synchrotron-based in-situ/operando XRD studies were conducted on 1C-NCAO and NCO cathodes. The cells, consisting of Na metal and a cathode composite, were cycled 100 times at 0.2C prior to the measurements. The XRD patterns for 1C-NCAO showed that the phase transitions from O3 to O3/O′3, O′3, O′3/P′3, and P′3 during charge were the same as those in pristine NCO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>A). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> The phase transitions during discharge were slightly delayed but still highly reversible, indicating excellent reversibility of 1C-NCAO. In contrast, the structural evolution of NCO was distinctively different (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>B). The XRD pattern of NCO at a fully discharged state showed the coexistence of the O3, O′3, and P′3 phases. Upon charging, the O3 phase converted to O′3, forming a O′3/P′3 mixed phase, and eventually resulted in a single P′3 phase. Discharge also showed phase evolution in the opposite direction (P′3 → O′3/P′3 → O3/O′3/P′3), with the same retardation as in 1C-NCAO. The persistence of the P′3 phase during the entire C/D process was interesting because it indicated that the formation of an inactive P′3 phase, which does not occur in NCAO, is a major cause of capacity loss in NCO. Full in-situ XRD patterns can be found in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S7 and S8</a>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Synchrotron-based in-situ XRD patterns during charge and discharge of (A) 1C-NCAO and (B) NCO. Prior to the measurements, the cells were subjected to 100 C/D cycles at 0.2C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It is worth noting that the formation of ternary phases (O3/O′3/P′3) in desodiated NCO has not been previously reported. Previous studies on irreversible phase transitions in NCO have mostly focused on the irreversible phase changes at high voltages. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref39" aria-label="reference 17 and 39">(17,39)</a> The relationship between capacity degradation and structural transformation in repeatedly cycled NCO below 3.6 V was unknown. Recently, Geng et al. monitored paramagnetic signal changes in the electrolyte solution during the charging of NCO and found that Cr disproportionation on the surface (Cr<sup>3+</sup> + Cr<sup>4+</sup> → Cr<sup>2+</sup> + Cr<sup>5+</sup>) leads to Cr<sup>5+</sup> dissolution when the high-voltage cutoff is beyond 3.75 V, causing irreversibility. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> We speculated the same process may occur, even when the high-voltage cutoff is limited to 3.6 V, due to repeated C/D cycles. To confirm this speculation, we examined the variation of Cr concentrations in the electrolyte solution using XPS. A drop of the electrolyte solution after C/D cycles was spin-coated on a slide glass, and a Cr spectrum for vacuum-dried powders was obtained. Because the peak intensity can be affected by the penetration depth of X-rays and the escape depth of photoelectrons (note that the sample was not a dense and homogeneous film), the intensity of Cr<sub>2p</sub> was normalized with respect to C<sub>1s</sub>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>A illustrates the change in normalized intensities of Cr<sub>2p</sub> in electrolytes (dried on slide glass), which were recovered from NCO and 1C-NCAO over a series of C/D cycles. It is obvious that the intensities of Cr<sub>2p</sub> in NCO increase with the number of C/D cycles, while those in NCAO are negligible. This strongly suggests that chromium dissolution from NCO occurs during repeated cycling. We also examined the Cr<sub>2p</sub> XPS spectra of cycled NCO and 1C-NCAO electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>B). The clear distinction was observed with an additional peak at lower binding energies in cycled NCO, which indicated the presence of Cr<sup>2+</sup> ions in the cycled NCO electrode in contrast to the absence in the cycled 1C-NCAO electrode. Once Cr<sup>5+</sup> ions are dissolved through the disproportionation reaction, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> large-sized Cr<sup>2+</sup> ions appear to migrate to prismatic sites in a Na layer, which stabilize the P′3 phases and eventually lose electroactivity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>C). Further research on the detailed mechanism of Cr<sup>2+</sup> migration is ongoing, and the results of this work will be presented in a future publication.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (A) XPS spectra of Cr<sub>2p</sub> of electrolytes (dried on slide glass), which were recovered from NCO|Na and 1C-NCAO|Na cells, demonstrating a gradual increase in chromium concentrations in NCO with cycling, in contrast to the absence of chromium ions. (B) XPS spectra of Cr<sub>2p</sub> on cycled NCO and 1C-NCAO electrodes at charged state (100 C/D), demonstrating the presence of Cr<sup>2+</sup> in cycled NCO electrode but the absence in cycled 1C-NCAO electrode. (C) Schematic illustration showing the relationship between chromium dissolution and the persistence of inactive P′3 phases.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Inertness of 1C-NCAO in Humid Air and Aqueous Solution</h3><div class="NLM_p">It is well-known that NIB cathodes with a layered structure, such as NCO, are sensitive to humid air. Water molecules can be intercalated into the Na layer, causing expansion of the <i>c</i>-axis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 41 to 43">(41−43)</a> Surface modification was an effective approach to alleviate this behavior in layered cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> While the primary aim of this study was to create a highly cyclable NCO through simultaneous coating and doping, we also evaluated the air stability of 1C-NCAO. The powdery samples were exposed to ambient air (humidity = ca. 3000 ppm) alongside NCO for comparison. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>A compares the evolution of XRD patterns of NCO and 1C-NCAO over 24 h and shows a clear difference between the two. While 1C-NCAO shows no changes in the patterns, new peaks begin to appear in NCO after 3 h of exposure and grow over time, corresponding to water-intercalated NCO and surface byproduct Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42 ref43 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 41 to 43, 45 and 46">(41−43,45,46)</a> Because NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> also shows new peaks after 24 h of exposure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), the complete inertness of 1C-NCAO in ambient air strongly suggests that the thin Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell functions as an efficient barrier against water penetrations.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (A) Evolution of XRD patterns of NCO and 1C-NCAO upon exposure to ambient air. (B) Thermogravimetric curves and (C) FTIR spectra of NCO and 1C-NCAO, exposed to air for 24 h. (D) Photos showing the differences between NCO and 1C-NCAO, when soaked in water.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The inertness of 1C-NCAO, in contrast to the hydration of NCO, was also confirmed through TGA (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>B) and FTIR (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>C). Two samples were left in air for 24 h and heated to 900 °C. As expected, the weight loss of 1C-NCAO during heating was minimal (99.4%), consistent with the results from XRD. In contrast, NCO showed significant and continuous weight loss between 100 and 600 °C (5.8 and 12.6%), attributed to the release of surface water (−5.8%) and the dehydration of water molecules intercalated in the Na layers (−12.6%), respectively. Based on the weight loss, the number of water molecules intercalated per formula unit in NCO was determined to be 0.9 (i.e., NaCrO<sub>2</sub>·0.9H<sub>2</sub>O), which was slightly higher than in P2-type layered cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref47" aria-label="reference 41 and 47">(41,47)</a> FTIR spectra also supported the stability of 1C-NCAO in humid air. While NCO showed the peaks characteristic of surface-adsorbed water (1460 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and Cr(III)–O–H bending (866 cm<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> no signs of water were observed in Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and 1C-NCAO. The ability of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shells to act as a water barrier in ambient air was also observed in aqueous solutions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>D). When soaked in water, NCO particles seemed to show an affinity for water, but their initial dispersion resulted in gradual settling, leaving a yellowish-green supernatant, likely due to the adsorption of water on the surface of NCO (slow settling) and the dissolution of chromium ions (color change). We also observed a significant increase in pH over time in a solution containing NCO, with the pH rising from 7.03 to 11.55 after 1 h and 12.25 after 24 h. This increase is due to the Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> exchange that occurs in most O3-type Na layered materials (NaMO<sub>2</sub> + <i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O → Na<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>H<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> + <i>x</i>NaOH). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> In contrast, we did not observe any signs of interaction (quick precipitation) or reaction with water (no color change) in a solution containing 1C-NCAO, and the pH changes were relatively small (from 7.03 to 7.15 and 7.22), indicating that Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> layers also serve as an effective water barrier in aqueous solutions. The reason for the slight color change observed in 1C-NCAO powder over time may be attributed to the formation of a thin hydrated oxide layer on the surface of the material. This color change is likely a result of light scattering and absorption by the hydrated layer. While a slight color change was observed after soaking 1C-NCAO in water for 24 h, there was no indication of structural changes, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">NCO with a core–shell structure was synthesized in a single pot in order to improve its cyclic stability. Spectroscopic and microscopic methods confirmed the simultaneous formation of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shells and Al-doped NCO cores. A thin layer of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (5 nm) and a small amount of Al substitution (Na(Cr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>)) were enough to provide NCO with the desired synergistic effects. 1C-NCAO showed no capacity decay during 1000 C/D cycles (107 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5C) and no kinetic retardation. In contrast to NCO, which showed the presence of inactive P′3 phases (O3/O′3/P′3 ↔ O′3/P′3 ↔ P′3) after repeated cycles, 1C-NCAO retained its characteristic phase transition behaviors (O3 ↔ O3/O′3 ↔ O′3 ↔ O′3/P′3 ↔ P′3). The differences between 1C-NCAO and NCO were also evident in the concentrations of chromium ions dissolved during C/D cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In addition, 1C-NCAO remained inert in humid air while NCO exhibited an expanded interslab distance and the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. Durability of 1C-NCAO in aqueous solutions was also confirmed. The solution pH of 1C-NCAO remained nearly invariant, in contrast to the abrupt increase in pH of a solution containing NCO.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c23236?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c23236</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of <i>x</i>C-NCAO; Rietveld fitting profiles and tables for fitting results; full in-situ XRD patterns of multiple-cycled NCO and 1C-NCAO during C/D; XRD of NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub> exposed to air for 24 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf">am2c23236_si_001.pdf (856.88 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c23236" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201703772"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nayak, P. Chem., Int. Ed.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>57</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">102</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">120</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/anie.201703772</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D57%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D102-120%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%252C%2BInt.%2BEd.%26author%3DP.%2BK.%2BNayak%26author%3DL.%2BYang%26author%3DW.%2BBrehm%26author%3DP.%2BAdelhelm%26title%3DFrom%2BLithium-Ion%2Bto%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253B%2BAdvantages%252C%2BChallenges%252C%2Band%2BSurprises%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.201703772&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c23236&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.201703772&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">From Lithium-Ion to Sodium-Ion Batteries: Advantages, Challenges, and Surprises</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nayak, Prasant Kumar; Yang, Liangtao; Brehm, Wolfgang; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">102-120</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C9CS00846B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanion-type Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>49</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2342</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2377</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C9CS00846B</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D49%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D2342-2377%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DT.%2BJin%26author%3DH.%2BLi%26author%3DK.%2BZhu%26author%3DP.-F.%2BWang%26author%3DP.%2BLiu%26author%3DL.%2BJiao%26title%3DPolyanion-type%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC9CS00846B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c23236&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC9CS00846B&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin Ting; Li Huangxu; Zhu Kunjie; Wang Peng-Fei; Liu Pei; Jiao Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2342-2377</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems (EESs) due to their significant benefits of abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  Among the previously reported cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition-metal oxides and polyanion-type materials are considered to be the most attractive options.  Although many layered transition-metal oxides can provide high capacity due to their small molecular weight, their further application is hindered by low output voltage (mostly lower than 3.5 V), irreversible phase transition as well as storage instability.  Comparatively, polyanion-type materials exhibit higher operating potentials due to the inductive effect of polyanion groups.  Their robust 3D framework significantly decreases the structural variations during sodium ion de/intercalation.  Moreover, the effect of strong X-O (X = S, P, Si, etc.) covalent bonds can effectively inhibit oxygen evolution.  These advantages contribute to the superior cycle stability and high safety of polyanion-type materials.  However, low electronic conductivity and limited capacity still restrict their further application.  This review summarizes the recent progress of polyanion-type materials for SIBs, which include phosphates, fluorophosphates, pyrophosphates, mixed phosphates, sulfates, and silicates.  We also discuss the remaining challenges and corresponding strategies for polyanion-type materials.  We hope this review can provide some insights into the development of polyanionic materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/batt.202200473"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Composite Oxide Cathode Materials: Phase Regulation, Electrochemical Performance and Reaction Mechanism</span>. <i>Batteries Supercaps</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/batt.202200473</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26publication_year%3D2022%26journal%3DBatteries%2BSupercaps%26author%3DX.%2BZhu%26author%3DS.-W.%2BXu%26author%3DX.-Z.%2BWang%26author%3DM.%2BLiu%26author%3DY.%2BCheng%26author%3DP.-F.%2BWang%26title%3DSodium%2BComposite%2BOxide%2BCathode%2BMaterials%253A%2BPhase%2BRegulation%252C%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformance%2Band%2BReaction%2BMechanism%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fbatt.202200473&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c23236&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fbatt.202200473&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03215B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoo, J.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, S.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, Y.-U.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goddard, W. A.,  III</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Unexpected Discovery of Low-cost Maricite NaFePO<sub>4</sub> as a High-Performance Electrode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">540</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">545</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4EE03215B</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D540-545%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DJ.%2BKim%26author%3DD.-H.%2BSeo%26author%3DH.%2BKim%26author%3DI.%2BPark%26author%3DJ.-K.%2BYoo%26author%3DS.-K.%2BJung%26author%3DY.-U.%2BPark%26author%3DW.%2BA.%2BGoddard%26author%3DK.%2BKang%26title%3DUnexpected%2BDiscovery%2Bof%2BLow-cost%2BMaricite%2BNaFePO4%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Performance%2BElectrode%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC4EE03215B&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c23236&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC4EE03215B&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unexpected discovery of low-cost maricite NaFePO4 as a high-performance electrode for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Jongsoon; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Kim, Hyungsub; Park, Inchul; Yoo, Jung-Keun; Jung, Sung-Kyun; Park, Young-Uk; Goddard, William A., III; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">540-545</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Battery chem. based on earth-abundant elements has great potential for the development of cost-effective, large-scale energy storage systems.  Herein, we report, for the first time, that maricite NaFePO4 can function as an excellent cathode material for Na ion batteries, an unexpected result since it has been regarded as an electrochem. inactive electrode for rechargeable batteries.  Our investigation of the Na re-(de)intercalation mechanism reveals that all Na ions can be deintercalated from the nano-sized maricite NaFePO4 with simultaneous transformation into amorphous FePO4.  Our quantum mechanics calcns. show that the underlying reason for the remarkable electrochem. activity of NaFePO4 is the significantly enhanced Na mobility in the transformed phase, which is ∼one fourth of the hopping activation barrier.  Maricite NaFePO4, fully sodiated amorphous FePO4, delivered a capacity of 142 mA h g-1 (92% of the theor. value) at the first cycle, and showed outstanding cyclability with a negligible capacity fade after 200 cycles (95% retention of the initial cycle).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/C5RA12158B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kee, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dimov, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Staikov, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Minami, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okada, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insight Into the Limited Electrochemical Activity of NaVP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub></span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">64991</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">64996</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability. Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Layered-to-Rock-Salt Transformation in Desodiated NaxCrO2 (x 0.4)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bo, Shou-Hang; Li, Xin; Toumar, Alexandra J.; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1419-1429</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">O3 layered sodium transition metal oxides (i.e., NaMO2, M = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni) are a promising class of cathode materials for Na-ion battery applications.  These materials, however, all suffer from severe capacity decay when the extn. of Na exceeds certain capacity limits.  Understanding the causes of this capacity decay is crit. to unlocking the potential of these materials for battery applications.  In this work, we investigate the structural origins of capacity decay for one of the compds. in this class, NaCrO2.  The (de)sodiation processes of NaCrO2 were studied both in situ and ex situ through X-ray and electron diffraction measurements.  We demonstrate that NaxCrO2 (0 &lt; x &lt; 1) remains in the layered structural framework without Cr migration up to a compn. of Na0.4CrO2.  Further removal of Na beyond this compn. triggers a layered-to-rock-salt transformation, which converts P'3-Na0.4CrO2 into the rock-salt CrO2 phase.  This structural transformation proceeds via the formation of an intermediate O3 NaδCrO2 phase that contains Cr in both Na and Cr slabs and shares very similar lattice dimensions with those of rock-salt CrO2.  It is intriguing to note that intercalation of alk. ions (i.e., Na+ and Li+) into the rock-salt CrO2 and O3 NaδCrO2 structures is actually possible, albeit in a limited amt. (∼0.2 per formula unit).  When these results were analyzed under the context of electrochem. data, it was apparent that preventing the layered-to-rock-salt transformation is crucial to improve the cyclability of NaCrO2.  Possible strategies for mitigating this detrimental phase transition are proposed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2012.06.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.-N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Z.-W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cycle Performance Improvement of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> Cathode by Carbon Coating for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">O3/P2-layered oxides, esp. O3-NaCrO2, have been widely investigated as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries due to their high stability and flexible synthesis procedure, but their low electron cond., the toxicity of Cr(VI) and cation disorder limit their applications.  Here, O3-Na(Cr0.8Mn0.2)O2 is reported as a promising cathode, which has a higher av. storage voltage than O3-NaCrO2 and a practical useable capacity of ca. 100 mAh/g.  The low vol. change (∼0.90%), lack of toxic Cr6+ formation and cation ordering in the optimized O3-Na(Cr,Mn)O2 structure ensure a potentially long cycle life. XAS and DFT investigations revealed variations in the local structure and the sequence of charge transfer of the transition metal during cycling.  This work sheds light on the sodium storage mechanism of O3-layered oxides from micro- and electronic-structure points of view and reveals the role of transition metals in the working mechanism and performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1039/D0RA08332A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, W.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">NaCrO2 with high rate-capability is an attractive cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (NIBs).  However, the amt. of reversibly extractable Na+ ions is restricted by half, which results in relatively low energy d. for practical NIB cathodes.  Herein, we describe aliovalent-doped O3-Na0.9[Cr0.9Sn0.1]O2 (NCSnO) and O3-Na0.8[Cr0.9Sb0.1]O2 (NCSbO), both of which show high-voltage characteristics that translate to an increase in energy d.  In contrast to NaCrO2, NCSnO and NCSbO can be reversibly charged to 3.80 and 3.95 V, resp., delivering 0.5 Na+ along with Cr3+/4+ redox alone.  The reversible chargeability to Na0.4[Cr0.9Sn0.1]O2 and Na0.3[Cr0.9Sb0.1]O2 is not assocd. with the suppression of Cr6+ formation.  Both compds. show concns. of Cr6+ that are higher than that of Na0.3CrO2, with an absence of O3' phases.  This implies that aliovalent-doping contributes to a suppression of the Cr6+ migration into tetrahedral sites in the interslab space, which reduces the possibility of irreversible comproportionation.  NCSnO and NCSbO deliver capacities comparable to that of NaCrO2, but show a higher av. discharge voltage (2.94 V for NaCrO2; 3.14 V for NCSnO; 3.21 V for NCSbO), which leads to a noticeable increase in energy densities.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A high-performance layered Cr-Based cathode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xi, Kaiying; Chu, Shufen; Zhang, Xiaoyu; Zhang, Xueping; Zhang, Haoyang; Xu, Hang; Bian, Jingjing; Fang, Tiancheng; Guo, Shaohua; Liu, Pan; Chen, Mingwei; Zhou, Haoshen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are the popular alternative for grid-scale energy storage due to the abundant resources and wide distribution of sodium.  However, NaCrO2, one of the most promising layered oxides for cathode materials, suffers from a serious capacity decrease by charging to the potential higher than 3.6 V, attributed to the irreversible chromium ions migration from CrO6 slabs to sodium layers during the initial charge process.  Herein we synthesize a novel O3-type layered Na0.88Cr0.88Ru0.12O2 material, which could be well operated at elevated potential 3.8 V.  The Na0.88Cr0.88Ru0.12O2 electrode delivers an extended reversible discharge capacity of 156 mAh g-1 and superior rate capability.  Moreover, Na0.88Cr0.88Ru0.12O2 electrode shows excellent cycling performance, illustrated by 80.7% capacity retention over 1100 cycles.  The results of high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy measurement indicate that the undesired migration of Cr ions during sodium extn. can be suppressed via Ru doping, which can significantly improve the electrochem. properties of Na0.88Cr0.88Ru0.12O2 compared to NaCrO2.  Our findings provide new insights for the common transition-metal migration during cycling layered cathodes, and motivate a new design strategy for high-performance SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2021.134305"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yue, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, R.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.-N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Suppressing O3-O’3 Phase Transition in NaCrO<sub>2</sub> Cathode Enabling High Rate Capability for Sodium-Ion Batteries by Sb Substitution</span>. <i>Chem. Improving rate capability for SIBs is a crit. issue.  Herein, we reveal that introducing small amt. of Sb into O3-NaCrO2 can improve the rate capability of NaCrO2 cathode significantly.  The occupation of Sb in Cr sites can expand the c/a ratio and the unit cell vol., thus promoting sodium diffusion in the a-b channels. Synchrotron in situ X-ray diffraction results reveals highly reversible phase transition behavior of NCS-0.05 during cycling even under high rates, and the O3 → O'3 phase transition in NaCrO2 is suppressed effectively by Sb substitution.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ-Formed Cr2O3 Coating on NaCrO2 with Improved Sodium Storage Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Shuo; Chen, Fei; Zhu, Tian-yuan; He, Xiao-dong; Liao, Jia-ying; Zhang, Li-ming; Ding, Xiang; Hu, Qiao; Chen, Chun-hua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cr2O3 is generally considered as an impurity phase with neg. effects on the electrochem. performance of NaCrO2 because it may cause a certain degree of capacity loss.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Improvement of the Cathode Electrolyte Interphase on P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 by Atomic Layer Deposition</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvarado, Judith; Ma, Chuze; Wang, Shen; Nguyen, Kimberly; Kodur, Moses; Meng, Ying Shirley</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Atomic layer deposition (ALD) is a commonly used coating technique for lithium ion battery electrodes.  Recently, it has been applied to sodium ion battery anode materials.  ALD is known to improve the cycling performance, Coulombic efficiency of batteries, and maintain electrode integrity.  Here, the electrochem. performance of uncoated P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 electrodes is compared to that of ALD-coated Al2O3 P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 electrodes.  Given that ALD coatings are in the early stage of development for NIB cathode materials, little is known about how ALD coatings, in particular aluminum oxide (Al2O3), affect the electrode-electrolyte interface.  Therefore, full characterizations of its effects are presented in this work.  For the first time, XPS is used to elucidate the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) on ALD-coated electrodes.  It contains less carbonate species and more inorg. species, which allows for fast Na kinetics, resulting in significant increase in Coulombic efficiency and decrease in cathode impedance.  The effectiveness of Al2O3 ALD coating is also surprisingly reflected in the enhanced mech. stability of the particle which prevents particle exfoliation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.3389/fenrg.2022.847818"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xue, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">MgO-Coated Layered Cathode Oxide with Enhanced Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Front.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-Cost Al-Doped Layered Cathodes with Improved Electrochemical Performance for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Feng, Yi-Hu; Cheng, Zhiwei; Xu, Chen-Liang; Yu, Lianzheng; Si, Duo; Yuan, Boheng; Liu, Mengting; Zhao, Bin; Wang, Peng-Fei; Han, Xiaogang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">23465-23473</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
