            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">O3-NaNi0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25O2 layered oxide is considered one of the most promising cathode candidates for sodium-ion batteries because of its advantages, such as its large capacity and low cost.  However, the practical application of this material is limited by its poor cyclic stability and insufficient rate capability.  Here, a strategy to substitute the Fe3+ in NaNi0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25O2 with Al3+ is adopted to address these issues.  The substitution of Fe3+ with Al3+ enhances the framework stability and phase transition reversibility of the parent NaNi0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25O2 material by forming a stronger TM-O bond, which improves the cycling stability.  Moreover, partial Al3+ substitution increases the interslab distance, providing a spacious path for Na+ diffusion and resulting in fast diffusion kinetics, which lead to improved rate capability.  Consequently, the target NaNi0.25Fe0.5-xAlxMn0.25O2 sample with optimal x = 0.045 exhibits a remarkable electrochem. performance in a Na-ion cell with a large reversible capacity of 131.7 mA h g-1, a stable retention of approx. 81.6% after cycling at 1C for 100 cycles, and a rate performance of 81.3 mA h g-1 at 10C.  This method might pave the way for novel means of improving the electrochem. properties of layered transitional-metal oxides and provide insightful guidance for the design of low-cost cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1039/D1NH00585E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Challenges of Layer-Structured Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nanoscale Horiz.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">338</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">351</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Challenges of layer-structured cathodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shi, Caihong; Wang, Liguang; Chen, Xi'an; Li, Jun; Wang, Shun; Wang, Jichang; Jin, Huile</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale Horizons</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  As the most promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) still face many issues that hinder their large-scale commercialization.  Layered transition metal oxide cathodes have attracted widespread attention owing to their large specific capacity, high ionic cond., and feasible prepn. conditions.  However, their electrochem. properties are usually limited by the irreversible phase transition and harsh storage conditions caused by humidity sensitivity.  Recently, tremendous efforts have been devoted to solving these issues toward advanced high-performance layered oxide cathodes.  Herein, we summarize these remaining challenges of layered oxide cathodes and the corresponding modification strategies such as the variations in chem. compns., the architecture of (nano)micro-structures, surface engineering, and the regulation of phase compns.  We hope that the understanding presented in this review can provide useful guidance to developing high-performance layer-structured cathode materials for advanced SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202117728"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, F.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Rational Biphasic Tailoring Strategy Enabling High Performance Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered oxide cathodes usually exhibit high compositional diversity, thus providing controllable electrochem. performance for Na-ion batteries.  These abundant components lead to complicated structural chem., closely affecting the stacking preference, phase transition and Na+ kinetics.  With this perspective, we explore the thermodynamically stable phase diagram of various P2/O3 composites based on a rational biphasic tailoring strategy.  Then a specific P2/O3 composite is investigated and compared with its monophasic counterparts.  A highly reversible structural evolution of P2/O3-P2/O3/P3-P2/P3-P2/Z/O3'-Z/O3' based on the Ni2+/Ni3.5+, Fe3+/Fe4+ and Mn3.8+/Mn4+ redox couples upon sequential Na extn./insertion is revealed.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Despite their high specific capacity, sodium layered oxides suffer from severe capacity fading when cycled at higher voltages.  This key issue must be addressed in order to develop high-performance cathodes for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  Herein, we present a comprehensive study on the influence of Al doping of Mn sites on the structural and electrochem. properties of a P2-Na0.5Mn0.5-xAlxCo0.5O2 (x = 0, 0.02, or 0.05) cathode for SIBs.  Detailed structural, morphol., and electrochem. investigations were carried out using X-ray diffraction, cyclic voltammetry, and galvanostatic charge-discharge measurements, and some new insights are proposed.  Rietveld refinement confirmed that Al doping caused TMO6 octahedra (TM = transition metal) shrinkage, resulting in wider interlayer spacing.  After optimizing the aluminum concn., the cathode exhibited remarkable electrochem. performance, with better stability and improved rate performance.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed at various states of charge to probe the surface and bulk effects of Al doping.  The material presented here exhibits exceptional stability over 100 cycles within a 1.5-4.3 V window and outperforms several other Mn-Co-based cathodes for SIBs.  This study presents a facile method for designing structurally stable cathodes for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1021/acsanm.7b00207"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tsuchiya, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Glushenkov, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effect of nanosizing on the sodium storage performance in NaCrO2 is systematically examd. The observation by high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy reveals that secondary particles consist of highly cryst. and nanosized NaCrO2 primary particles with enriched grain boundaries.  Such morphol. features affect the voltage profiles in sodium cells, leading to an S-shaped profile with a single-phase reaction even for layered NaCrO2, in which a biphasic reaction dominates because of a large repulsive interaction between Na ions.  Moreover, the O3-P3 phase transition is suppressed for the heat-treated sample with the presence of enriched grain boundaries.  The suppression of the phase transition is proposed to be due to the cancellation of CrO2 layers gliding for the incoherently aligned grain boundaries.  Thus, good capacity retention as electrode materials is realized compared with as-prepd. bulk O3 NaCrO2.  Nanotechnol. potentially changes materials design strategies for sodium insertion materials, leading to the development of innovative rechargeable sodium batteries in the future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b20149"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Denis, D. Herein, we devise a scaled-up electrospinning avenue to fabricate nanocrystal-constructed ultralong layered NaCrO2 nanowires (NWs) toward SIBs as a wide-temp.-operating cathode.         </div><div class="casAbstract">NaCrO2 is a potential cathode material for sodium-ion batteries due to its low cost, safety, and high power.  It is necessary to further understand its electronic property during cycling in advance of practical application.  In this work, operando EPR is carried out to monitor the evolution of the electronic structure for NaCrO2 cycled between 2.2-3.6 V and 2.2-4.5 V.  We discover that electronic delocalization takes place at the early stage of charge, which may account for the excellent rate performance.  In addn., via EPR imaging, an EPR signal assocd. with the irreversible phase transition at 3.8 V is located in the electrolyte, which is then attributed to the Cr5+ ions dissolved with the surface reconstruction.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium layered-oxide cathodes show promise as an alternative to lithium layered oxides for grid-scale energy storage applications.  Among the many sodium layered-oxide chemistries, O3-type structures with a mixt. of nickel, manganese, and iron are of interest due to their high theor. energy d.  The main challenges of these materials include poor cycle life and air stability due to surface reactivity in both electrolytes and ambient moisture.  Surface modifications can mitigate both challenges by protecting the underlying cathode material.  Phosphate coatings are of particular interest due to their low cost and high cond., but they have not yet been explored for O3 sodium layered-oxide materials.  Furthermore, the relative benefits of different coating methods and phosphate groups are not well-understood.  Herein, the electrochem. performance and air stability of two coating methods are compared, and their unique challenges with an O3 sodium layered oxide are explored.  Unlike lithium layered oxides or P2-type sodium layered oxides, O3 sodium layered oxides suffer from irreversible extn. of sodium during the coating process resulting in metal oxide formation.  This sodium extn. causes reduced capacity and cond. at high coating contents and highlights the unique challenges of O3-type sodium layered oxides.  Despite these challenges, a 1% (NaPO3)n coating achieves significant improvement over the uncoated material, corresponding to a 14% gain in capacity retention after 100 cycles and only a loss of 30 mA h g-1 after 9 days in humid air, compared to 80 mA h g-1 lost in the uncoated sample.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-020-17290-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zuo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ortiz, G.                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The stability of P2-layered sodium transition metal oxides in ambient atmospheres</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zuo, Wenhua; Qiu, Jimin; Liu, Xiangsi; Ren, Fucheng; Liu, Haodong; He, Huajin; Luo, Chong; Li, Jialin; Ortiz, Gregorio F.; Duan, Huanan; Liu, Jinping; Wang, Ming-Sheng; Li, Yangxing; Fu, Riqiang; Yang, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Air-stability is one of the most important considerations for the practical application of electrode materials in energy-harvesting/storage devices, ranging from solar cells to rechargeable batteries.  The promising P2-layered sodium transition metal oxides (P2-NaxTmO2) often suffer from structural/chem. transformations when contacted with moist air.  However, these elaborate transitions and the evaluation rules towards air-stable P2-NaxTmO2 have not yet been clearly elucidated.  Herein, taking P2-Na0.67MnO2 and P2-Na0.67Ni0.33Mn0.67O2 as key examples, we unveil the comprehensive structural/chem. degrdn. mechanisms of P2-NaxTmO2 in different ambient atmospheres by using various microscopic/spectroscopic characterizations and first-principle calcns.  The extent of bulk structural/chem. transformation of P2-NaxTmO2 is detd. by the amt. of extd. Na+, which is mainly compensated by Na+/H+ exchange.  By expanding our study to a series of Mn-based oxides, we reveal that the air-stability of P2-NaxTmO2 is highly related to their oxidn. features in the first charge process and further propose a practical evaluating rule assocd. with redox couples for air-stable NaxTmO2 cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.8b03637"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dolocan, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Understanding the Air-Exposure Degradation Chemistry at a Nanoscale of Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>19</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">182</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">188</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Understanding the Air-Exposure Degradation Chemistry at a Nanoscale of Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">You, Ya; Dolocan, Andrei; Li, Wangda; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">19</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">182-188</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Undesired reactions between layered Na transition-metal oxide cathodes and air impede their utilization in practical Na-ion batteries.  Consequently, a fundamental understanding of how layered oxide cathodes degrade in air is of paramount importance, but it has not been fully understood yet.  Here, a comprehensive study on a model material NaNi0.7Mn0.15Co0.15O2 reveals its reaction chem. with air and the dynamic evolution of the degrdn. species upon air exposure.  We find that besides the extn. of Na+ from the crystal lattice to form NaOH, Na2CO3, and Na2CO3·H2O in contact with air, Ni ions gradually dissolve from the bulk to form NiO and accumulate on the particle surface as revealed by subnanometer surface-sensitive time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectroscopy.  The degrdn. species on the surface are insulating, leading to an increase in interfacial resistance and declined electrochem. performance.  We also demonstrate a feasible surface coating strategy for suppressing the unfavorable degrdn. process.  Understanding the degrdn. mechanism at a nanoscale can facilitate the future development of high-energy cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref46" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.1c00047"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Che, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Degradation Mechanism of O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> Cathode Materials during Ambient Storage and their In Situ Regeneration</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2061</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2067</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsaem.1c00047</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D4%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D2061-2067%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DY.%2BSun%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DD.%2BMeng%26author%3DX.%2BLi%26author%3DX.%2BLiao%26author%3DH.%2BChe%26author%3DG.%2BCui%26author%3DF.%2BYu%26author%3DW.%2BYang%26author%3DL.%2BLi%26author%3DZ.-F.%2BMa%26title%3DDegradation%2BMechanism%2Bof%2BO3-type%2BNaNi1%252F3Fe1%252F3Mn1%252F3O2%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bduring%2BAmbient%2BStorage%2Band%2Btheir%2BIn%2BSitu%2BRegeneration%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsaem.1c00047&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c23236&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsaem.1c00047&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Degradation Mechanism of O3-Type NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 Cathode Materials During Ambient Storage and Their In Situ Regeneration</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Yang; Wang, Hong; Meng, Dechao; Li, Xiaoqiao; Liao, Xiaozhen; Che, Haiying; Cui, Guijia; Yu, Fengping; Yang, Weimin; Li, Linsen; Ma, Zi-Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2061-2067</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAEMCQ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) hold great promise for low-cost energy storage.  Despite the major advances made in the material prepn. and battery performance, air instability has become a bottleneck for the storage and electrode fabrication of O3-type NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 (NFM), but the underlying mechanism remains elusive.  Here we discovered that NFM loses Na+ ions during ambient storage and Na2CO3 "fibers" sprout from the particle surface, which caused the performance decay.  We further demonstrated a facile resintering strategy to regenerate the NFM in situ.  This work highlights the importance of stringent humidity control and provides the basis for designing surface-modification strategies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.3389/fchem.2019.00335"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Air-Stable Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> Cathodes for Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Front.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Air-stable NaxTMO2 cathodes for sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yi; Zhang, Renyuan; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Frontiers in Chemistry (Lausanne, Switzerland)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Frontiers Media S.A.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries are considered to be the most promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale stationary energy storage applications due to the abundant sodium resource in the Earth' crust and as a result, relatively low cost.  Sodium layered transition metal oxides (NaxTMO2) are proper Na-ion cathode materials because of low cost and high theor. capacity.  Currently most researchers focus on the improvement of electrochem. performance such as high rate capability and long cycling stability.  However, for NaxTMO2, the structure stability against humid atm. is essentially important since most of them are instable in air, which is not favorable for practical application.  Here we provide a comprehensive review of recent progresses on air-stable NaxTMO2 oxides.  Several effective strategies are discussed, and further investigations on the air-stable cathodes are prospected.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1080/17518253.2016.1151083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sone, B.                 10.1016/j.materresbull.2013.03.027</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D48%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D2678-2686%26journal%3DMater.%2BRes.%2BBull.%26author%3DE.%2BMonyoncho%26author%3DR.%2BBissessur%26title%3DUnique%2Bproperties%2Bof%2B%25CE%25B1-NaFeO2%253A%2BDe-Intercalation%2Bof%2BSodium%2Bvia%2BHydrolysis%2Band%2Bthe%2BIntercalation%2Bof%2BGuest%2BMolecules%2Binto%2Bthe%2BExtract%2BSolution%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.materresbull.2013.03.027&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c23236&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.materresbull.2013.03.027&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unique properties of α-NaFeO2: De-intercalation of sodium via hydrolysis and the intercalation of guest molecules into the extract solution</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Monyoncho, Evans; Bissessur, Rabin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Research Bulletin</span>

                        cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review-Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubota, Kei; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research on electrochem. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.  Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.  Although research interests of the electrode materials for Na-ion batteries are evoked in many researchers, advantages, disadvantages, and issues are not fully discussed for realizing the commercialization of Na-ion batteries. The red dots, black line, and blue line represent observed, calculated, and difference profiles, respectively. (C) Evolution of lattice dimensions with <i>x</i> in <i>x</i>C-NCAO and mol % of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> obtained from the refinements.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (A) XPS spectra of Na, Cr, and Al for NCO and <i>x</i>C-NCAO. (B) STEM image and corresponding EDX maps of 1C-NCAO, showing the homogeneous distribution of constituents and quantitative agreement of relative atomic % with nominal compositions. (C) High-resolution TEM images of 1C-NCAO, showing the presence of a surface shell with a thickness of 5 nm. (D) ED patterns on the periphery (square in (C)), demonstrating that the surface layer consists of Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Representative voltage profiles for (A) NCO, (B) 0.025Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-coated NCO, (C) NaCr<sub>0.95</sub>Al<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, (D) 2.5C-NCAO, (E) 1C-NCAO, and (F) 5C-NCAO during 100 C/D cycles at 0.2C. (G) Comparison of long-term cyclability of 1C-NCAO and NCO at 5C. Comparison of rate capability of (H) 1C-NCAO and (I) NCO. Cells were subjected to 100 C/D cycles at 5C prior to rate tests in (H) and (I). Solid and open marks indicate charge and discharge capacities, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Synchrotron-based in-situ XRD patterns during charge and discharge of (A) 1C-NCAO and (B) NCO. Prior to the measurements, the cells were subjected to 100 C/D cycles at 0.2C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0005.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (A) XPS spectra of Cr<sub>2p</sub> of electrolytes (dried on slide glass), which were recovered from NCO|Na and 1C-NCAO|Na cells, demonstrating a gradual increase in chromium concentrations in NCO with cycling, in contrast to the absence of chromium ions. (B) XPS spectra of Cr<sub>2p</sub> on cycled NCO and 1C-NCAO electrodes at charged state (100 C/D), demonstrating the presence of Cr<sup>2+</sup> in cycled NCO electrode but the absence in cycled 1C-NCAO electrode. (C) Schematic illustration showing the relationship between chromium dissolution and the persistence of inactive P′3 phases.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig6"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/medium/am2c23236_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0006.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr6" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (D) Photos showing the differences between NCO and 1C-NCAO, when soaked in water.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/asset/images/large/am2c23236_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c23236&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></li><li id="pane-m2" aria-labelledby="pane-m2" class="tab__pane tab__pane-references"><div class="refs-header-label"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i15">References</h2></div></div></div><hr></div><p class="rightTab-references-count">

                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201703772"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nayak, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C9CS00846B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanion-type Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>49</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2342</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2377</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C9CS00846B</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin Ting; Li Huangxu; Zhu Kunjie; Wang Peng-Fei; Liu Pei; Jiao Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2342-2377</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems (EESs) due to their significant benefits of abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  Among the previously reported cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition-metal oxides and polyanion-type materials are considered to be the most attractive options.  Although many layered transition-metal oxides can provide high capacity due to their small molecular weight, their further application is hindered by low output voltage (mostly lower than 3.5 V), irreversible phase transition as well as storage instability.  Comparatively, polyanion-type materials exhibit higher operating potentials due to the inductive effect of polyanion groups.  Their robust 3D framework significantly decreases the structural variations during sodium ion de/intercalation.  Moreover, the effect of strong X-O (X = S, P, Si, etc.) covalent bonds can effectively inhibit oxygen evolution.  These advantages contribute to the superior cycle stability and high safety of polyanion-type materials.  However, low electronic conductivity and limited capacity still restrict their further application.  This review summarizes the recent progress of polyanion-type materials for SIBs, which include phosphates, fluorophosphates, pyrophosphates, mixed phosphates, sulfates, and silicates.  We also discuss the remaining challenges and corresponding strategies for polyanion-type materials.  We hope this review can provide some insights into the development of polyanionic materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1002/batt.202200473"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Composite Oxide Cathode Materials: Phase Regulation, Electrochemical Performance and Reaction Mechanism</span>. <i>Batteries Supercaps</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Battery chem. based on earth-abundant elements has great potential for the development of cost-effective, large-scale energy storage systems.  Herein, we report, for the first time, that maricite NaFePO4 can function as an excellent cathode material for Na ion batteries, an unexpected result since it has been regarded as an electrochem. inactive electrode for rechargeable batteries.  Our investigation of the Na re-(de)intercalation mechanism reveals that all Na ions can be deintercalated from the nano-sized maricite NaFePO4 with simultaneous transformation into amorphous FePO4.  Our quantum mechanics calcns. show that the underlying reason for the remarkable electrochem. activity of NaFePO4 is the significantly enhanced Na mobility in the transformed phase, which is ∼one fourth of the hopping activation barrier.  Maricite NaFePO4, fully sodiated amorphous FePO4, delivered a capacity of 142 mA h g-1 (92% of the theor. value) at the first cycle, and showed outstanding cyclability with a negligible capacity fade after 200 cycles (95% retention of the initial cycle).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1039/C5RA12158B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kee, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dimov, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Staikov, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Minami, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okada, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insight Into the Limited Electrochemical Activity of NaVP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub></span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">64991</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">64996</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, LiVP2O7 has been investigated as a possible high-voltage substitute for Li2FeP2O7.  However, its Na-equiv., NaVP2O7, as an economic replacement for Li2FeP2O7 has not yet been well understood.  Here, for the first time, we report the feasibility of NaVP2O7 as a 3.4 V cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  Having a theor. capacity of 108 mA h g-1, it shows an initial discharge capacity of 38.4 mA h g-1 at 1/20C (1C = 108 mA g-1) in the voltage range of 2.5-4.0 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries are being considered as promising cost-effective energy storage devices for the future compared to Li-ion batteries owing to the crustal abundance of Na-ion.  However, the large radius of the Na ion result in sluggish electrode kinetics that leads to poor electrochem. performance, which prohibits the use of these batteries in real time application.  Therefore, identification and optimization of the anode, cathode, and electrolyte are essential for achieving high-performance Na-ion batteries.  In this context, the current review discusses the suitable high-voltage cathode materials for Na-ion batteries.  According to a recent research survey, sodium vanadium fluorophosphate (NVPF) compds. have been emphasized for use as a high-voltage Na-ion cathode material.  Among the fluorophosphate groups, Na3V2(PO4)2F3 exhibited the high theor. capacity (128 mAh g-1) and working voltage (∼3.9 V vs. Na/Na+) compared to the other fluorophosphates and Na3V2(PO4)3.  Here, we have also highlighted the classification of Fluorophosphates, NVPF composite with carbonaceous materials, the appropriate synthesis methods and how these methods can enhance the electrochem. performance.  Finally, the recent developments in NVPF for the application in energy storage devices and its outlook are summarized.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201903968"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A High-Energy NASICON-type Cathode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Over the last decade, Na-ion batteries have been extensively studied as low-cost alternatives to Li-ion batteries for large-scale grid storage applications; however, the development of high-energy pos. electrodes remains a major challenge.  Materials with a polyanionic framework, such as Na superionic conductor (NASICON)-structured cathodes with formula NaxM2(PO4)3, have attracted considerable attention because of their stable 3D crystal structure and high operating potential.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.  This paper presents a brief review of the research progress of polyanion-type electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, summarizing recent accomplishments, highlighting emerging strategies, and discussing the remaining challenges of such systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles. Understanding the causes of this capacity decay is crit. to unlocking the potential of these materials for battery applications.  In this work, we investigate the structural origins of capacity decay for one of the compds. in this class, NaCrO2.  The (de)sodiation processes of NaCrO2 were studied both in situ and ex situ through X-ray and electron diffraction measurements.  We demonstrate that NaxCrO2 (0 &lt; x &lt; 1) remains in the layered structural framework without Cr migration up to a compn. of Na0.4CrO2.  Further removal of Na beyond this compn. triggers a layered-to-rock-salt transformation, which converts P'3-Na0.4CrO2 into the rock-salt CrO2 phase.  This structural transformation proceeds via the formation of an intermediate O3 NaδCrO2 phase that contains Cr in both Na and Cr slabs and shares very similar lattice dimensions with those of rock-salt CrO2.  It is intriguing to note that intercalation of alk. ions (i.e., Na+ and Li+) into the rock-salt CrO2 and O3 NaδCrO2 structures is actually possible, albeit in a limited amt. (∼0.2 per formula unit).  When these results were analyzed under the context of electrochem. data, it was apparent that preventing the layered-to-rock-salt transformation is crucial to improve the cyclability of NaCrO2.  Possible strategies for mitigating this detrimental phase transition are proposed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2012.06.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.-N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Z.-W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cycle Performance Improvement of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> Cathode by Carbon Coating for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochem. However, NaCrO2, one of the most promising layered oxides for cathode materials, suffers from a serious capacity decrease by charging to the potential higher than 3.6 V, attributed to the irreversible chromium ions migration from CrO6 slabs to sodium layers during the initial charge process.  Herein we synthesize a novel O3-type layered Na0.88Cr0.88Ru0.12O2 material, which could be well operated at elevated potential 3.8 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Atomic layer deposition (ALD) is a commonly used coating technique for lithium ion battery electrodes.  Recently, it has been applied to sodium ion battery anode materials.  ALD is known to improve the cycling performance, Coulombic efficiency of batteries, and maintain electrode integrity.  Here, the electrochem. performance of uncoated P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 electrodes is compared to that of ALD-coated Al2O3 P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 electrodes.  Given that ALD coatings are in the early stage of development for NIB cathode materials, little is known about how ALD coatings, in particular aluminum oxide (Al2O3), affect the electrode-electrolyte interface.         </div><div class="casAbstract">O3-NaNi0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25O2 layered oxide is considered one of the most promising cathode candidates for sodium-ion batteries because of its advantages, such as its large capacity and low cost.  However, the practical application of this material is limited by its poor cyclic stability and insufficient rate capability.  Here, a strategy to substitute the Fe3+ in NaNi0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25O2 with Al3+ is adopted to address these issues.  The substitution of Fe3+ with Al3+ enhances the framework stability and phase transition reversibility of the parent NaNi0.25Fe0.5Mn0.25O2 material by forming a stronger TM-O bond, which improves the cycling stability.  Moreover, partial Al3+ substitution increases the interslab distance, providing a spacious path for Na+ diffusion and resulting in fast diffusion kinetics, which lead to improved rate capability.  Consequently, the target NaNi0.25Fe0.5-xAlxMn0.25O2 sample with optimal x = 0.045 exhibits a remarkable electrochem. performance in a Na-ion cell with a large reversible capacity of 131.7 mA h g-1, a stable retention of approx. 81.6% after cycling at 1C for 100 cycles, and a rate performance of 81.3 mA h g-1 at 10C.  This method might pave the way for novel means of improving the electrochem. properties of layered transitional-metal oxides and provide insightful guidance for the design of low-cost cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1039/D1NH00585E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Challenges of Layer-Structured Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nanoscale Horiz.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">338</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">351</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  As the most promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) still face many issues that hinder their large-scale commercialization.  Layered transition metal oxide cathodes have attracted widespread attention owing to their large specific capacity, high ionic cond., and feasible prepn. conditions.  However, their electrochem. properties are usually limited by the irreversible phase transition and harsh storage conditions caused by humidity sensitivity.  Recently, tremendous efforts have been devoted to solving these issues toward advanced high-performance layered oxide cathodes.  Herein, we summarize these remaining challenges of layered oxide cathodes and the corresponding modification strategies such as the variations in chem. compns., the architecture of (nano)micro-structures, surface engineering, and the regulation of phase compns.  We hope that the understanding presented in this review can provide useful guidance to developing high-performance layer-structured cathode materials for advanced SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref35" class="rightTabRefNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit35" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202117728"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, F.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Rational Biphasic Tailoring Strategy Enabling High Performance Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew. With this perspective, we explore the thermodynamically stable phase diagram of various P2/O3 composites based on a rational biphasic tailoring strategy.  Then a specific P2/O3 composite is investigated and compared with its monophasic counterparts.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Despite their high specific capacity, sodium layered oxides suffer from severe capacity fading when cycled at higher voltages.  This key issue must be addressed in order to develop high-performance cathodes for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  Herein, we present a comprehensive study on the influence of Al doping of Mn sites on the structural and electrochem. properties of a P2-Na0.5Mn0.5-xAlxCo0.5O2 (x = 0, 0.02, or 0.05) cathode for SIBs. Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed at various states of charge to probe the surface and bulk effects of Al doping.  The material presented here exhibits exceptional stability over 100 cycles within a 1.5-4.3 V window and outperforms several other Mn-Co-based cathodes for SIBs.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effect of nanosizing on the sodium storage performance in NaCrO2 is systematically examd.  Cation-disordered rock-salt-type and nanosized NaCrO2 is prepd. by mech. milling, and layered O3-type and nanosized NaCrO2 is prepd. by heat treatment of the rock-salt phase.  The observation by high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy reveals that secondary particles consist of highly cryst. and nanosized NaCrO2 primary particles with enriched grain boundaries.  Such morphol. features affect the voltage profiles in sodium cells, leading to an S-shaped profile with a single-phase reaction even for layered NaCrO2, in which a biphasic reaction dominates because of a large repulsive interaction between Na ions.  Moreover, the O3-P3 phase transition is suppressed for the heat-treated sample with the presence of enriched grain boundaries. Thus, good capacity retention as electrode materials is realized compared with as-prepd. bulk O3 NaCrO2. Chem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">335</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.3389/fchem.2019.00335</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Air-stable NaxTMO2 cathodes for sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yi; Zhang, Renyuan; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Frontiers in Chemistry (Lausanne, Switzerland)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">335</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">FCLSAA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2296-2646</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Frontiers Media S.A.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries are considered to be the most promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale stationary energy storage applications due to the abundant sodium resource in the Earth' crust and as a result, relatively low cost.  Sodium layered transition metal oxides (NaxTMO2) are proper Na-ion cathode materials because of low cost and high theor. capacity.  Currently most researchers focus on the improvement of electrochem. performance such as high rate capability and long cycling stability.  However, for NaxTMO2, the structure stability against humid atm. is essentially important since most of them are instable in air, which is not favorable for practical application.  Here we provide a comprehensive review of recent progresses on air-stable NaxTMO2 oxides.  Several effective strategies are discussed, and further investigations on the air-stable cathodes are prospected.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1080/17518253.2016.1151083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sone, B. T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manikandan, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gurib-Fakim, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maaza, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Single-Phase α-Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> Nanoparticles’ Green Synthesis Using Callistemon Viminalis’ Red Flower Extract</span>. <i>Green Chem. Lett. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>9</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">85</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">90</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1080/17518253.2016.1151083</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Single-phase α-Cr2O3 nanoparticles' green synthesis using Callistemon viminalis' red flower extract</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sone, B. T.; Manikandan, E.; Gurib-Fakim, A.; Maaza, M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Green Chemistry Letters and Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">85-90</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">GCLRAI</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1751-7192</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Taylor &amp; Francis Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This contribution reports for the first time on the synthesis and the main phys. properties of single-phase pure α-Cr2O3 nanoparticles synthesized by a facile, rapid and eco-friendly process using Callistemon viminalis flower's ext. as an effective oxidizing/reducing agent.  These cryst. nanoparticles exhibit a cubic-like platelet shape with sharp edges with an av. particle diagonal size of ∼92.2 nm.  The room temp. phys. properties of these pure highly cryst. Eskolaite α-Cr2O3 nanoparticles were carried out using complementary techniques such as high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS), XRD, FTIR-ATR, Raman and XPS.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1016/j.materresbull.2013.03.027"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Monyoncho, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bissessur, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Unique properties of α-NaFeO<sub>2</sub>: De-Intercalation of Sodium via Hydrolysis and the Intercalation of Guest Molecules into the Extract Solution</span>. <i>Mater. Res. Bull.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>48</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2678</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2686</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.materresbull.2013.03.027</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unique properties of α-NaFeO2: De-intercalation of sodium via hydrolysis and the intercalation of guest molecules into the extract solution</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Monyoncho, Evans; Bissessur, Rabin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Research Bulletin</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">48</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2678-2686</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MRBUAC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0025-5408</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Authors report on a versatile method for the de-intercalation of Na from α-NaFeO2 by using water to produce α-Na1-xFeO2, where x ≈ 1.  This de-intercalation technique provides an excellent route to ion exchange Na with other metal ions in α-NaFeO2.  The hydrolysis mechanism is provided.  Also showed that the extd. soln. captures CO2 from the atm. leading to the formation of sodium hydrogen carbonate hydrate crystals.  The lamellar structure of the hydrate crystals was confirmed by powder x-ray diffraction, and were found Na-deficient via elemental anal.  Intercalation of guest mols. such as polymers, alcs., and inorg. ions into the gallery space of the newly formed sodium hydrogen carbonate hydrate crystals was demonstrated by the use of powder x-ray diffraction technique.  The reported materials were also characterized by Moessbauer spectroscopy, thermogravimetric anal., differential scanning calorimetry, IR spectroscopy, XPS, and SEM.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1149/2.0151514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review-Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Electrochem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>162</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">A2538</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">A2550</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/2.0151514jes</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review-Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubota, Kei; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">162</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A2538-A2550</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research on electrochem. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.  Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.  Although research interests of the electrode materials for Na-ion batteries are evoked in many researchers, advantages, disadvantages, and issues are not fully discussed for realizing the commercialization of Na-ion batteries.  In this article, practical issues and perspective are reviewed on the basis of mainly authors exptl. experiences, know-how, and results, and the future direction is proposed to overcome the issues and to challenge the advanced performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i12">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c23236?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c23236</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of <i>x</i>C-NCAO; Rietveld fitting profiles and tables for fitting results; full in-situ XRD patterns of multiple-cycled NCO and 1C-NCAO during C/D; XRD of NaCr<sub>0.98</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub> exposed to air for 24 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c23236/suppl_file/am2c23236_si_001.pdf">am2c23236_si_001.pdf (856.88 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();
